   552	
   553	I. Introduction
   554	
   555	Table of Contents
   556	1. Introduction
   557	
   558	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   559	
   560	Chapter 1. Introduction
   561	
   562	Introduction
   563	
   564	This is the Linux Standard Base Work In Progress 20030620 . An implementation
   565	of anything in this document may not claim to be an implementation of the Linux
   566	Standard Base until such time as the feature moves into the Linux Standards
   567	Base Specification.
   568	
   569	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   570	
   571	Purpose
   572	
   573	The Linux Standard Base (LSB) defines a system interface for compiled
   574	applications and a minimal environment for support of installation scripts. Its
   575	purpose is to enable a uniform industry standard environment for high-volume
   576	applications conforming to the LSB.
   577	
   578	The LSB defines a binary interface for application programs that are compiled
   579	and packaged for LSB-conforming implementations on many different hardware
   580	architectures. Since a binary specification must include information specific
   581	to the computer processor architecture for which it is intended, it is not
   582	possible for a single document to specify the interface for all possible
   583	LSB-conforming implementations. Therefore, the LSB is a family of
   584	specifications, rather than a single one.
   585	
   586	The LSB is composed of two basic parts: A common specification ("generic LSB"
   587	or "gLSB") describing those parts of the interface that remain constant across
   588	all implementations of the LSB, and an architecture-specific specification
   589	("archLSB") describing the parts of the interface that vary by processor
   590	architecture. Together, the generic LSB and the architecture-specific
   591	supplement for a single hardware architecture provide a complete interface
   592	specification for compiled application programs on systems that share a common
   593	hardware architecture.
   594	
   595	This document is the generic LSB. It must be used in conjunction with an
   596	architecture-specific supplement. Whenever a section of this specification must
   597	be supplemented by architecture-specific information, this document includes a
   598	reference to the architecture supplement. Architecture supplements may also
   599	contain additional information that is not referenced here.
   600	
   601	This document should be used in conjunction with the documents it references.
   602	This document enumerates the system components it includes, but descriptions of
   603	those components may be included entirely or partly in this document, partly in
   604	other documents, or entirely in other reference documents. For example, the
   605	section that describes system service routines includes a list of the system
   606	routines supported in this interface, formal declarations of the data
   607	structures they use that are visible to applications, and a pointer to the
   608	underlying referenced specification for information about the syntax and
   609	semantics of each call. Only those routines not described in standards
   610	referenced by this document, or extensions to those standards, are described in
   611	the detail. Information referenced in this way is as much a part of this
   612	document as is the information explicitly included here.
   613	
   614	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   615	
   616	Related Standards
   617	
   618	The specifications listed below are referenced in whole or in part by the Linux
   619	Standard Base. When a specification is referenced in a way that imposes a
   620	requirement (for example, "foo must behave as specified in the XyzzySpec"),
   621	then the relevant requirements of that specification apply as if they were part
   622	of the LSB itself. However, if the LSB refers to a specification without
   623	imposing a requirement, then it is merely a reference and does not add
   624	additional requirements. The LSB may refer to a portion of a specification (for
   625	example, to define a specific function or group of functions); in such cases,
   626	only the explicitly referenced portion of the specification applies.

   804	
   805	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   806	
   807	How to Use this Standard
   808	
   809	The complete LSB specification is composed of this generic LSB specification
   810	and the supplemental processor-specific specification for a particular
   811	processor architecture. These two documents constitute a specification that
   812	should be used in conjunction with the publicly-available standards documents
   813	it references. The LSB enumerates the system components it includes, but
   814	descriptions of those components may be included entirely in the LSB, partly in
   815	the LSB and partly in other documents, or entirely in other reference
   816	documents.
   817	
   818	II. Base Libraries
   819	
   820	Table of Contents
   821	2. Libraries
   822	
   823	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   824	
   825	Chapter 2. Libraries
   826	
   827	An LSB-conforming implementation shall support some base libraries which
   828	provide interfaces for accessing the operating system, processor and other
   829	hardware in the system.
   830	
   831	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   832	
   833	Interfaces for libstdcxx

   841	+------------------------+
   842	
   843	All symbols are in the std:: namespace.
   844	
   845	The behavior of the interfaces in this library is specified by the following
   846	standards.
   847	
   848	ISO/IEC 14882: 1998(E) Programming languages --C++[1]
   849	Linux Standard Base[2]

   853	18.4 C++ Dynamic Memory Managment
   854	
   855	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   856

  9857	Table 2-398. libstdcxx - Default C++ LibGroup should be empty when done Data
  9858	Interfaces
  9859	
  9860	+-----------------------------------------------------------------------+
  9861	|typeinfo for signed char const*[2]|typeinfo name for signed char const*|
  9862	+-----------------------------------------------------------------------+
  9863	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
  9864	
  9865	Data Definitions for libstdcxx
  9866	
  9867	This section contains standard data definitions that describe system data.
  9868	These definitions are organized into groups that correspond to system headers.
  9869	This convention is used as a convenience for the reader, and does not imply the
  9870	existence of these headers, or their content.
  9871	
  9872	ISO C serves as the LSB reference programming language, and data definitions
  9873	are specified in ISO C format. The C language is used here as a convenient
  9874	notation. Using a C language description of these data objects does not
  9875	preclude their use by other programming languages.
  9876	
  9877	III. System Initialization
  9878	
  9879	Table of Contents
  9880	3. System Initialization
  9881	
  9882	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
  9883	
  9884	Chapter 3. System Initialization
  9885	
  9886	Cron Jobs
  9887	
  9888	Packages may not touch the configuration file /etc/crontab, nor may they modify
  9889	the files in /var/spool/cron/crontabs.
  9890	
  9891	If a package wants to install a job that has to be executed via cron, it shall
  9892	place a file in one of the following directories:
  9893	
  9894	/etc/cron.daily
  9895	/etc/cron.weekly
  9896	/etc/cron.monthly
  9897	
  9898	As these directory names say, the files within them are executed on a daily,
  9899	weekly, or monthly basis, respectively. See below for the rules concerning the
  9900	names of these files.
  9901	
  9902	 If a certain job has to be executed more frequently than daily, the package
  9903	shall install a file /etc/cron.d/cron-name tagged as configuration file. This
  9904	file uses the same syntax as /etc/crontab and is processed by cron
  9905	automatically.
  9906	
  9907	It is recommended that files installed in any of these directories be scripts
  9908	(shell scripts, Perl scripts, etc.) so that they may be modified by the local
  9909	system administrator. In addition, they must be registered as configuration
  9910	file.
  9911	
  9912	The scripts in these directories have to check, if all necessary programs are
  9913	installed before they try to execute them. Otherwise, problems will arise when
  9914	a package was removed (but not purged), since the configuration files are kept
  9915	on the system in this situation.
  9916	
  9917	To avoid namespace conflicts in the /etc/cron.* directories, the filenames used
  9918	by LSB-compliant packages in /etc/cron.daily, /etc/cron.weekly, /etc/
  9919	cron.monthly, or /etc/cron.d must come from a managed namespace. These
  9920	filenames may be assigned using one of the following methods:
  9921	
  9922	  * Assigned namespace. This namespace consists of names which only use the
  9923	    character set [a-z0-9]. In order to avoid conflicts these cron script names
  9924	    must be reserved through the Linux Assigned Names and Numbers Authority
  9925	    (LANANA). Information about the LANANA may be found at www.lanana.org.
  9926	   
  9927	    Commonly used names shall be reserved in advance; developers for projects
  9928	    should be encouraged reserve names from LANA, so that each distribution can
  9929	    use the same name, and to avoid conflicts with other projects.
  9930	   
  9931	  * Hierarchical namespace. This namespace consists of scripts names which look
  9932	    like this: [hier1]-[hier2]-...-[name], where name is again taken the
  9933	    character set [a-z0-9], and where there may be one or more [hier-n]
  9934	    components. [hier1] may either be an LSB provider name assigned by the
  9935	    LANANA, or it may be owners' DNS name in lower case, with at least one '.'.
  9936	    I.e., "debian.org", "staroffice.sun.com", etc. The LSB provider name
  9937	    assigned by LANANA must only consist of the ASCII characters [a-z0-9].
  9938	   
  9939	  * Reserved namespace. This namespace consists of script names which begin
  9940	    with the character '_', and is reserved for distribution use only. This
  9941	    namespace should be used for core packages only, and in general use of this
  9942	    namespace is highly discouraged.
  9943	   
  9944	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
  9945	
  9946	Init Script Actions
  9947	
  9948	Init files provided by LSB applications shall accept one argument, saying what
  9949	to do:
  9950	
  9951	start        start the service                                                 
  9952	stop         stop the service                                                  
  9953	restart      stop and restart the service if the service is already running,   
  9954	             otherwise start the service                                       
  9955	try-restart  restart the service if the service is already running             
  9956	reload       cause the configuration of the service to be reloaded without     
  9957	             actually stopping and restarting the service                      
  9958	force-reload cause the configuration to be reloaded if the service supports    
  9959	             this, otherwise restart the service                               
  9960	status       print the current status of the service                           
  9961	
  9962	The start, stop, restart, force-reload, and status commands must be supported
  9963	by all init files; the reload and the try-restart options are optional. Other
  9964	init script actions may be defined by the init script.
  9965	
  9966	Init files must ensure that they will behave sensibly if invoked with start
  9967	when the service is already running, or with stop when it isn't, and that they
  9968	don't kill unfortunately-named user processes. The best way to achieve this is
  9969	to use the init-script functions provided by /lib/lsb/init-functions.
  9970	
  9971	If a service reloads its configuration automatically (as in the case of cron,
  9972	for example), the reload option of the init file must behave as if the
  9973	configuration has been reloaded successfully.
  9974	
  9975	These executable files must not fail obscurely when the configuration files
  9976	remain but the package has been removed, as the default in [the packaging
  9977	system] is to leave configuration files on the system after the package has
  9978	been removed. Only when it is executed with the [purge] option will [the
  9979	packaging system] remove configuration files. Therefore, you should include a
  9980	test statement at the top of the file, like this:
  9981	test -f program-executed-later-in-file || exit 5
  9982	or take the equivalent action if the init file is not a shell script.
  9983	
  9984	If the status command is given, the init script will return the following exit
  9985	status codes.
  9986	
  9987	0       program is running or service is OK           
  9988	1       program is dead and /var/run pid file exists  
  9989	2       program is dead and /var/lock lock file exists
  9990	3       program is not running                        
  9991	4       program or service status is unknown          
  9992	5-99    reserved for future LSB use                   
  9993	100-149 reserved for distribution use                 
  9994	150-199 reserved for application use                  
  9995	200-254 reserved                                      
  9996	
  9997	In the case of init script commands other than "status" (i.e., "start", "stop",
  9998	"restart", "reload", and "force-reload"), the init script must return an exit
  9999	status of zero if the action described by the argument has been successful.
 10000	Otherwise, the exit status shall be non-zero, as defined below. In addition to
 10001	straightforward success, the following situations are also to be considered
 10002	successful:
 10003	
 10004	  * restarting a service (instead of reloading it) with the "force-reload"
 10005	    argument
 10006	   
 10007	  * running "start" on a service already running
 10008	   
 10009	  * running "stop" on a service already stopped or not running
 10010	   
 10011	  * running "restart" on a service already stopped or not running
 10012	   
 10013	 
 10014	
 10015	In case of an error, while processing any init script action except for
 10016	"status", the init script must print an error message and return one of the
 10017	following non-zero exit status codes.
 10018	
 10019	1       generic or unspecified error (current practice)
 10020	2       invalid or excess argument(s)                  
 10021	3       unimplemented feature (for example, "reload")  
 10022	4       user had insufficient privilege                
 10023	5       program is not installed                       
 10024	6       program is not configured                      
 10025	7       program is not running                         
 10026	8-99    reserved for future LSB use                    
 10027	100-149 reserved for distribution use                  
 10028	150-199 reserved for application use                   
 10029	200-254 reserved                                       
 10030	
 10031	Error and status messages should be printed with the logging functions such as
 10032	log_failure_msg and so on. Scripts may write to standard error or standard
 10033	output, but implementations need not present text written to standard error/
 10034	output to the user or do anything else with it.
 10035	
 10036	Since init files may be run manually by a system administrator with
 10037	non-standard environment variable values for PATH, USER, LOGNAME, etc. init
 10038	files must not depend on the values of these environment variables. They should
 10039	set them to some known/default values if they are needed. 
 10040	
 10041	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
 10042	
 10043	Comment conventions for init scripts
 10044	
 10045	LSB applications which need to execute script(s) at bootup and/or shutdown may
 10046	provide one or more init.d files. These files are installed by the
 10047	install_initd program described below, which copies it into a standard
 10048	directory and makes whatever other adjustments (creation of symlinks, creation
 10049	of entries in a database, etc.) are necessary so that the script can be run at
 10050	boot-time. [3]
 10051	
 10052	In the init.d file, information about the shell script shall be delimited by
 10053	the lines "### BEGIN INIT INFO" and "### END INIT INFO". These delimiter lines
 10054	may containg trailing whitespace, which shall be ignored. Inside this block
 10055	there shall be lines of the form "# {keyword}: [arg1] [arg2] ...". (All lines
 10056	inside this block start with a hash ('#') character in the first column, so
 10057	that shell treats them as comments.) There must be exactly one space character
 10058	between "#" and the keyword.[4] The following keywords, with their arguments
 10059	are defined in this specification:
 10060	
 10061	        # Provides: boot_facility_1 [ boot_facility_2 ...]
 10062	        # Required-Start: boot_facility_1 [ boot_facility_2 ...]
 10063	        # Required-Stop: boot_facility_1 [ boot_facility_2 ...]
 10064	        # Should-Start: boot_facility_1 [ boot_facility_2 ...]
 10065	        # Should-Stop: boot_facility_1 [ boot_facility_2 ...]
 10066	        # Default-Start: run_level_1 [ run_level_2 ...]
 10067	        # Default-Stop: run_level_1 [ run_level_2 ...]
 10068	        # Short-Description: short_description
 10069	        # Description: multiline_description
 10070	
 10071	Additional keywords may be defined in future LSB specifications. Distributions
 10072	may define local extensions by using the prefix "X-[distribution name]" --- for
 10073	example, "X-RedHat-foobardecl", or "X-Debian-xyzzydecl".
 10074	
 10075	An init.d shell script may declare using the "Required-Start: " header that it
 10076	must not be run until certain boot facilities are provided. This information is
 10077	used by the installation tool or the boot-time boot-script execution facility
 10078	to assure that init scripts are run in the correct order. When an init script
 10079	is run with a "start" argument, the boot facility or facilities specified in
 10080	the "Provides" header shall be considered present, and hence init scripts which
 10081	require those boot facilities would then be eligble to be run. When an init
 10082	script is run with a "stop" argument, the boot facilities specified in the
 10083	"Provides" header are considered no longer present. There are naming
 10084	conventions for boot facilities and system facilities, as described in a
 10085	following section.
 10086	
 10087	Similarly, the "Required-Stop:" header defines which facilities must still be
 10088	available during the shutdown of that service. Hence, the init script system
 10089	should avoid stopping shell scripts which provide those facilities until this
 10090	shell script is stopped.
 10091	
 10092	The "Should-Start:" header defines which facilities if present should be
 10093	started before this service. This allows for weak dependencies which do not
 10094	cause the service to fail if a facility is not available. But may cause reduced
 10095	functionality of the service. Compliant applications should not rely on the
 10096	existence of this feature.
 10097	
 10098	The "Should-Stop:" header defines which facilities should be still available
 10099	during the shutdown of that service.
 10100	
 10101	The "Default-Start" and "Default-Stop" headers define which run levels should
 10102	by default run the script with a start or stop argument, respectively, to start
 10103	or stop the services controlled by the init script.
 10104	
 10105	The "Short-Description" and "Description" header fields are used to provide
 10106	text which describes the actions of the init script. The "short_description"
 10107	shall be a relatively short, pithy description of the init script, where as the
 10108	"multiline_description" can be a much longer piece of text that may span
 10109	mulitple lines. In a multiline description, each continuation line must begin
 10110	with a '#' followed by tab character or a '#' followed by at least two space
 10111	characters. The multiline description is terminated by the first line that does
 10112	not match this criteria.
 10113	
 10114	The comment conventions described in this session are only required for use by
 10115	LSB-compliant applications; system init scripts as provided by LSB-compliant
 10116	run-time environments are not required to use the scheme outlined here.
 10117	
 10118	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
 10119	
 10120	Installation and removal of init.d files
 10121	
 10122	An init.d file is installed by copying it into /etc/init.d (which may be a
 10123	symlink to another location). This can be done by the package installer. During
 10124	the package's postinstall script, the program "/usr/lib/lsb/install_initd"
 10125	configures the distribution's boot script system to call the package's init.d
 10126	file at the appropriate time.
 10127	
 10128	The install_initd program takes a single argument, the pathanme to the /etc/
 10129	init.d file. For example:
 10130	
 10131	        /usr/lib/lsb/install_initd /etc/init.d/example.com-coffeed
 10132	
 10133	When a software package is removed, the package's preuninstall script shall
 10134	call /usr/lib/lsb/remove_initd and pass the pathname to the /etc/init.d file.
 10135	The package manager is still responsible for removing the /etc/init.d file; the
 10136	remove_initd program is provided in case the distribution needs to clean up any
 10137	other modifications in the distribution's boot script system that might have
 10138	been made by the install_initd program. For example:
 10139	
 10140	        /usr/lib/lsb/remove_initd /etc/init.d/example.com-coffeed
 10141	
 10142	There should be a tool available to the user (e.g., RedHat's chkconfig) which
 10143	can be used by the system administrator to easily manipulate at which init
 10144	levels a particular init.d script is started or stopped. This specification
 10145	currently does not specify such an interface, however.
 10146	
 10147	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
 10148	
 10149	Run Levels
 10150	
 10151	 The following run levels are specified for use by the "Default-Start:" and
 10152	"Default-Stop:" specifiers as defined by the section Comment conventions for
 10153	init scripts>. Many LSB run-time environments commonly use these run level
 10154	definitions, and in the absence of other considerations, providers of run-time
 10155	environments are strongly encouraged to follow this convention to provide
 10156	consistency for system administrators who need to work with multiple
 10157	distributions. However, it is not required that LSB-compliant run-time
 10158	environments use these run levels; the distribution-provided install_initd
 10159	script may map the run levels specified below to whatever
 10160	distribution-specified run levels are most appropriate. 
 10161	
 10162	0 halt                                                    
 10163	1 single user mode                                        
 10164	2 multiuser with no network services exported             
 10165	3 normal/full multiuser                                   
 10166	4 reserved for local use, default is normal/full multiuser
 10167	5 multiuser with xdm or equivalent                        
 10168	6 reboot                                                  
 10169	
 10170	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
 10171	
 10172	Facility names
 10173	
 10174	Boot facilities are used to indicate dependencies in init scripts, as defined
 10175	in a previous section. Facility names that begin with a dollar sign ('$') are
 10176	system facility names, defined by the LSB, and MUST be provided by
 10177	distributions. [5] LSB applications must not provide facilities that begin with
 10178	a dollar sign. This document defines the following facility names:
 10179	
 10180	$local_fs  all local filesystems are mounted                                   
 10181	$network   low level networking (ethernet card; may imply PCMCIA running)      
 10182	$named     daemons which may provide hostname resolution (if present) are      
 10183	           running                                                             
 10184	$portmap   daemons providing SunRPC/ONCRPC portmapping service[6] (if present) 
 10185	           are running                                                         
 10186	$remote_fs all remote filesystems are mounted[7].                              
 10187	$syslog    system logger is operational                                        
 10188	$time      the system time has been set [8]                                    
 10189	
 10190	Other (non-system) facilities may be defined by other LSB applications. These
 10191	facilities shall be named using the same conventions defined for naming init.d
 10192	script names. Commonly, the facility provided by an LSB application init.d
 10193	script will have the same name as the name assigned to the init.d script.
 10194	
 10195	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
 10196	
 10197	Script names
 10198	
 10199	Since the init.d scripts must live in a single directory, they must come from a
 10200	single namespace. Three means of assigning names from this namespace are
 10201	available:
 10202	
 10203	  * Assigned namespace. This namespace consists of names which only use the
 10204	    character set [a-z0-9]. This space is desirable for scripts which system
 10205	    administrators may often wish to run manually: e.g., "/etc/init.d/named
 10206	    restart" In order to avoid conflicts these init.d names must be reserved
 10207	    through the Linux Assigned Names and Numbers Authority (LANANA).
 10208	    Information about the LANANA may be found at www.lanana.org.
 10209	   
 10210	    Commonly used names shall be reserved in advance; developers for projects
 10211	    should be encouraged to reserve names from LANANA, so that each
 10212	    distribution can use the same name, and to avoid conflicts with other
 10213	    projects.
 10214	   
 10215	  * Hierarchical namespace. This namespace consists of scripts names which look
 10216	    like this: [hier1]-[hier2]-...-[name], where name is again taken the
 10217	    character set [a-z0-9], and where there may be one or more [hier-n]
 10218	    components. [hier1] may either be an LSB provider name assigned by the
 10219	    LANANA, or it may be owners' DNS name in lower case, with at least one '.'
 10220	    (e.g., "debian.org", "staroffice.sun.com"). The LSB provider name assigned
 10221	    by LANANA must only consist of the ASCII characters [a-z0-9].
 10222	   
 10223	  * Reserved namespace. This namespace consists of script names which begin
 10224	    with the character '_', and is reserved for distribution use only. This
 10225	    namespace should be used for core packages only, and in general use of this
 10226	    namespace is highly discouraged.  
 10227	   
 10228	In general, if a package or some system function is likely to be used on
 10229	multiple systems, the package developers or the distribution SHOULD get a
 10230	registered name through LANANA, and distributions should strive to use the same
 10231	name whenever possible. For applications which may not be "core" or may not be
 10232	commonly installed, the hierarchical namespace may be more appropriate. An
 10233	advantage to the hierarchical namespace is that there is no need to consult
 10234	with the LANANA before obtaining an assigned name.
 10235	
 10236	Short names are highly desirable, since many system administrators like to use
 10237	them to manually start and stop services. Given this, they should be
 10238	standardized on a per-package basis. This is the rationale behind having a
 10239	LANANA organization to assign these names. The LANANA may be called upon to
 10240	handle other namespace issues, such as package/prerequisites naming (which is
 10241	essential to making prerequisites to work correctly).
 10242	
 10243	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
 10244	
 10245	Init script Functions
 10246	
 10247	Each LSB-compliant init.d script must source the file /lib/lsb/init-functions.
 10248	This file must cause the following shell script commands to be defined. This
 10249	can be done either by adding a directory to the PATH variable which defines
 10250	these commands, or by defining sh aliases. While the distribution-provided
 10251	aliases may choose to use bash extensions (at the distribution's option), the
 10252	LSB init.d files themselves should only depend in /bin/sh features as defined
 10253	by POSIX.2.
 10254	
 10255	                This runs the specified program as a daemon. start_daemon will 
 10256	start_daemon    check to see if there is a program named "daemon" already      
 10257	[-f] [-n        running. If so, it will not start another copy of the daemon   
 10258	nicelevel] [-p  unless the -f option is given. The -n option specifies a nice  
 10259	pidfile]        level. See nice(1). start_daemon should return the LSB defined 
 10260	pathname [args] exit status codes. It shall return 0 if the program has been   
 10261	                successfully started or is running and not 0 otherwise.        
 10262	                This stops the specified program. The program is found using   
 10263	                the algorithm given by pidofproc. If a signal is specified,    
 10264	                using the -signal_name or -signal_number syntaxes specified by 
 10265	killproc [-p    the kill command, the program is sent that signal. Otherwise, a
 10266	pidfile]        SIGTERM followed by a SIGKILL after some number of seconds is  
 10267	pathname        sent. Compliant applications may use the basename instead of   
 10268	[signal]        the pathname. killproc should return the LSB defined exit      
 10269	                status codes. If called without a signal, it shall return 0 if 
 10270	                the program has been stopped or is not running and not 0       
 10271	                otherwise. If a signal is given, it shall return 0 only if the 
 10272	                program is running.                                            
 10273	                This function returns one or more pid(s) for a particular      
 10274	                daemon. If an entry is found in /var/run/basename.pid, then    
 10275	                that value is returned. If the -p option is used, the given    
 10276	                pidfile is used instead. Compliant applications may use the    
 10277	                basename instead of the pathname. Compliant implementations of 
 10278	                the LSB may attempt other mechanisms for determining the pid   
 10279	pidofproc [-p   (s), although this is not required (and not recommended, since 
 10280	pidfile]        a user can trick startup scripts by creating processes that    
 10281	pathname        appear to be system programs in the process list thus creating 
 10282	                a potential security exposure). Hence, LSB-complaint           
 10283	                applications who wish to use the pidofproc function in their   
 10284	                init scripts must store the pid in /var/run/basename.pid.      
 10285	                pidofproc should return the LSB defined exist status codes for 
 10286	                "status". It shall return 0 if the program is the process is   
 10287	                running and not 0 otherwise.                                   
 10288	log_success_msg This requests the distribution to print a success message. The 
 10289	"message"       message should be relatively short; no more than 60 characters 
 10290	                is highly desirable.                                           
 10291	log_failure_msg This requests the distribution to print a failure message. The 
 10292	"message"       message should be relatively short; no more than 60 characters 
 10293	                is highly desirable.                                           
 10294	log_warning_msg This requests the distribution to print a warning message. The 
 10295	"message"       message should be relatively short; no more than 60 characters 
 10296	                is highly desirable.                                           
 10297	
 10298	Notes
 10299	
 10300	[1]  ISO/IEC 14882: 1998(E) Programming languages --C++                        
 10301	                                                                               
 10302	[2]  Linux Standard Base                                                       
 10303	                                                                               
 10304	[3]  This specification does not require, but is designed to allow, the        
 10305	     development of a system which runs boot scripts in parallel. Hence,       
 10306	     enforced-serialization of scripts is avoided unless it is explicitly      
 10307	     necessary.                                                                
 10308	                                                                               
 10309	[4]  More than one space, or a tab character, indicates the continuation line. 
 10310	                                                                               
 10311	[5]  The dollar sign does not indicate variable expansion as in many Linux     
 10312	     utilities. Starting a facility name with a dollar sign is merely a way of 
 10313	     dividing the namespace between the system and applications.               
 10314	                                                                               
 10315	[6]  as defined in RFC 1833                                                    
 10316	                                                                               
 10317	[7]  In some LSB run-time environments, filesystems such as /usr may be remote.
 10318	     Many applications that require $local_fs will probably require also       
 10319	     require $remote_fs                                                        
 10320	                                                                               
 10321	[8]  i.e., using a network-based time program such as ntp or rdate, or via the 
 10322	     hardware Real Time Clock                                                  
 10323
